"sim": 0.6000000238418579, "url": "https://www.dailymail.co.uk/wires/pa/article-13522287/The-Green-Party-s-General-Election-manifesto-glance.html", "title": "The Green Party\u00b4s General Election manifesto at a glance", "body": "The Green Party have launched their General Election manifesto, with promises to stop \"all new fossil fuel projects\", overhaul the tax system and give \"rights to nature itself\".\n\nHere are some of the headline policy proposals from the Greens.\n\n- The economy\n\nThe Green Party has pledged to raise taxes for the wealthiest in society and mend \"broken Britain\" in its election manifesto.\n\nA tax on multimillionaires and billionaires will be used to raise additional revenue of between \u00a330-70 billion to help fund improvements to health, housing, transport and the green economy, the party said.\n\nIn its manifesto, the Greens also said \"privatisation has failed\" and promised to bring water companies, railways and the five big retail energy companies into state ownership.\n\nOther measures include a pledge to invest \u00a340 billion a year to shift to a green economy, a \u00a312.4 billion investment pledge in green skills and training, and a carbon tax to drive down emissions - all during the next parliament.\n\n(PA Graphics)\n\n- Energy\n\nThe Green Party said it will \"accelerate clean energy investment and delivery\" by cancelling recent oil and gas licences such as those for the Rosebank oil and gas field, removing all oil and gas subsidies and stopping all new fossil fuel extraction projects in the UK.\n\nThe party also said it wants to see \"the phase-out of all nuclear energy\" and will push for wind to provide 70% of UK energy by 2030.\n\n- Nature\n\nOn nature, the party has outlined extensive plans to protect and restore the UK's environment by \"giving rights to nature itself\".\n\nThe party will work to end the sewage scandal, extend people's access to nature with a new English Right to Roam Act, and end the emergency authorisation of bee-killing pesticides.\n\nOn protecting animals and habitats, elected MPs will call for an end to all blood sports, badger culling, factory farming, routine use of antibiotics in farm animals and close confinement in cages.\n\nThe Greens pledge to work with farmers to 'transform our food and farming system\u00b4 (Danny Lawson/PA)\n\n- Farming\n\nFor more on agriculture, the Greens have pledged to \"work with farmers and other stakeholders to transform our food and farming system\".\n\nTheir manifesto includes measures such as almost tripling financial support for farmers to transition to nature-friendly farming, improving biodiversity and soil health, and linking farm payments to reduced use of pesticides.\n\n- Housing\n\nThe party has vowed to provide \"fairer, greener homes for all\", signalling that it wants to make the UK's housing crisis one of its key priorities for the next Parliament.\n\nUnder their plans, housebuilders would have to include solar panels and heat pumps on all new homes where appropriate and \u00a333 billion would be invested in insulating homes and other buildings over the next five years.\n\nThe party also promised to provide 150,000 new social homes every year and a \"fair deal for renters\" by giving powers to local authorities to introduce rent control measures.\n\n- Health and social care\n\nOn health, the Greens said they want to ensure a \"fully public, properly funded health and social care system\", promising to spend \u00a350 billion per year by 2030.\n\nThe manifesto said elected MPs will push for a year-on-year reduction in waiting lists, guaranteed access to an NHS dentist, guaranteed rapid access to a GP, and an immediate boost to the pay of NHS staff.\n\nThe Green Party manifesto said elected MPs will push for guaranteed rapid access to a GP (Anthony Devlin/PA)\n\nOther areas the party will focus on include increasing funding for mental health care and support a change in the law to legalise assisted dying for those with terminal diseases.\n\nOn social care, the party has pledged to introduce free personal care, increase carer pay rates and introduce a career structure for the sector.\n\n- Workers' rights\n\nIn the manifesto, the party said it plans to \"defend and extend workers' rights\" as it outlined measures such as repealing \"anti-union\" legislation and introducing a maximum 10:1 pay ratio.\n\nThe party will also push to increase the minimum wage to \u00a315 an hour no matter the age - up from its current rate of \u00a311.44 an hour for those aged 21 or over - and will move to introduce a four-day working week.\n\n- Welfare\n\nIn the manifesto's section on social support, the party pledges to \"tackle the cost-of-living crisis for the poorest in our society\".\n\nMeasures include increasing Universal Credit and legacy benefits by \u00a340 a week, abolishing the two-child benefit cap, introducing a universal basic income and giving disability benefits an immediate 5% uplift.\n\n- Transport\n\nThe Greens' plans to cut carbon emissions include championing \"better, cheaper public transport\" by increasing annual public subsidies for rail and bus travel to \u00a310bn and investing an additional \u00a319 billion to improve public transport by the end of the next Parliament.\n\nOther measures include a focus on active travel, with the party pledging to invest \u00a32.5bn a year in new cycleways and footpaths, and reducing traffic in residential areas to boost community use.\n\nOn aviation, elected MPs will push for a frequent-flyer levy, a ban on domestic flights for journeys that would take less than three hours by train and a halt to the expansion of new airport capacity.\n\nThe party is pledging to invest \u00a32.5bn a year in new cycleways and footpaths (Ben Birchall/PA)\n\n- Education\n\nThe Greens said the current education system \"operates like a production line rather than valuing their individual qualities\".\n\nThe manifesto said elected MPs will champion an increase in school funding of \u00a38 billion, ending tuition fees for higher education students, stopping high-stakes testing at schools and abolishing Ofsted.\n\n- Arts and culture\n\nGreen MPs will push for a \u00a35 billion investment to support community sports, arts and culture, the manifesto said.\n\nThe party also backs efforts to keep local sports facilities, museums, theatres, libraries and art galleries open and thriving as well as ending VAT on cultural activities.\n\n- Media reform\n\nThe manifesto said the current media landscape is \"skewed by the dominance of billionaire and big-tech ownership\" with the aim of maximising profits.\n\nThe party said it will push for a change in the law so that no single individual or company can own more than 20% of any media market and introduce all reforms proposed in the second part of the 2012 Leveson Report.\n\n- Foreign policy\n\nThe Green Party said it will seek to rejoin the European Union \"as soon as the political conditions are right\" as it unveiled its foreign policy objectives.\n\nIn the manifesto, the party said UK foreign policy \"should be based on shared commitments to democracy, peace, global solidarity and the protection of human rights\" as it pledged continued support for Ukraine against the Russian invasion.\n\nOn Israel and Palestine, the party said it will push for an immediate bilateral ceasefire as well as a \"durable political solution\".\n\nOn nuclear weapons, the party said it would see the UK begin the process of dismantling its nuclear weapons, cancel the Trident programme and remove all foreign nuclear weapons from UK soil.\n\nAnd on supporting Global South countries to tackle the climate crisis, the Greens back an increase to international aid to 1% of gross national income (GNI) and an increase to climate finance to 1.5% by 2033.\n\n- Rights and freedoms\n\nThe manifesto said the party will \"defend the Human Rights Act, the UK's membership of the European Convention on Human Rights and continued direct access to convention rights in the domestic courts\".\n\nOn political reform, the party will replace the first past the post system for parliamentary elections with a fair and proportional voting system, replace the House of Lords with an elected second chamber and provide votes for 16-year-olds.\n\nThe party said it will also campaign to end violence against women and girls, scrap legislation that erodes the right to protest and free expression, and campaign for the right of self-identification for trans and non-binary people.\n\nOther headline pledges including working to renew England's crumbling court system with a \u00a32.5bn investment and giving Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland the choice to make their \"own decisions about their relationship with the rest of the United Kingdom\".\n\n- Immigration\n\nThe Green Party said it would replace the Home Office with a new Department of Migration (Rick Findler/PA)\n\nIn its manifesto, the Green Party said it welcomes \"the contributions that migrants and refugees make to British society\" and would support safe routes to sanctuary for those fleeing persecution.\n\nThe party also said it will replace a \"dysfunctional Home Office\" with a new Department of Migration, end immigration detention, and allow {"uri": "8174575588", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-12", "time": "16:01:56", "dateTime": "2024-06-12T16:01:56Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-12T16:00:53Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.5960784554481506, "url": "https://moneyweek.com/personal-finance/green-party-manifesto-2024-personal-finance-general-election-policies", "title": "Green Party manifesto 2024: key personal finance general election policies", "body": "The Green Party manifesto for the 2024 general election has been launched, with the party making several eye-catching pledges.\n\nSpeaking from Sussex County Cricket Club's ground in Hove, co-leaders Carla Denyer and Adrian Ramsay promised that a Green government would give nature legal rights and would renationalise water companies and the railways. Other pledges included plans to end the \"hostile environment\" faced by migrants and refugees, and to bring in a four-day working week.\n\nThe party also made several commitments that would affect personal finances, if it gets elected. As well as a wealth tax, it wants to implement a \"maximum 10:1 pay ratio\" for businesses and bring in a universal basic income.\n\nThe news comes after the Conservative Party unveiled its manifesto. On Tuesday (11 June), Rishi Sunak announced \u00a317bn in tax cuts, including a further cut to National Insurance and the introduction of the state pension triple lock plus.\n\nIt also follows the Liberal Democrat manifesto launch on Monday (10 June), during which Sir Ed Davey's party promised to make the UK a fairer place to live and work. Key pledges included closing capital gains tax loopholes and taxing share buybacks.\n\nLabour is set to publish its manifesto on Thursday (13 June). Sir Keir Starmer's party has already revealed a Freedom to Buy scheme and plans to add VAT to private school fees.\n\nThe Greens believe they can raise an extra \u00a350bn to \u00a370bn a year by reforming personal taxes. Pointing out that the number of billionaires in the UK has risen from 29 to 171 since 2010, they say they want to \"fairly tax excessive concentrations of wealth\" to fund public services and simplify the wider tax system.\n\nOne of its main policies is a new wealth tax. This levy would come in at a rate of 1% on all assets above \u00a310m and 2% on assets over \u00a31bn. The party has also targeted the wealthy by pledging to align the rates of income tax and capital gains tax (CGT). It also wants to align tax rates on investment income with income tax and National Insurance Contribution (NIC) rates for employees.\n\nA further change to NICs that would be brought in if the party gets elected would see the main 8% rate extended beyond the upper earnings limit (\u00a350,270). At present, anything above the threshold is taxed at 2%. This would mean that somebody earning \u00a355,000 a year would pay \u00a35.46 a week more in tax (\u00a3283.74 extra a year), while a worker on \u00a365,000 a year would pay an extra \u00a317 a week (\u00a3883 a year).\n\nThe Greens also want to equate the rate of pension tax relief with the basic rate of income tax. It says this means that everyone would only get a tax relief of 20%. The manifesto also includes a vague pledge to close inheritance tax loopholes that are exploited by the wealthy. Frequent flyers would face a punitive levy on their fares, while the party would seek to ban domestic flights where the journey can be undertaken in less than three hours by train.\n\nBut the manifesto does include one non-punitive tax measure. It says it would end VAT on cultural activities. This measure would lower the prices of, for example, museum admission and tickets to gigs in pubs.\n\nAway from personal finance, the party's tax pledges include the introduction of a carbon tax. Set at \u00a3120 per tonne of emissions before climbing to a maximum of \u00a3500 per tonne over a 10-year period, it expects the levy would raise an extra \u00a380bn. The policy, which is aimed at making decarbonisation a cheaper alternative for business compared to continuing with the status quo, would also initially align with other fossil fuel taxes, such as fuel duty and payments under the emissions trading scheme.\n\nThe manifesto promises to not hike the main rates of corporation tax, and says the Greens would continue a windfall tax on energy companies as well as bring in one on banks, which have benefited from high interest rates. These measures would raise \u00a39bn a year by the next election, they claim. The Greens would also advocate for further windfall taxes in other sectors if extra profits caused by \"market distortions\" come in as being \"risk free\".\n\nAs with the other parties, the Greens have earmarked more funding for HMRC to tackle tax avoidance and evasion. But, unlike the other parties, it has not set a figure for how much it plans to raise through this crackdown.\n\nThe Green manifesto suggests the party would move away from the triple lock to a double lock system. It says it would ensure that pensions are \"always uprated\" in line with inflation and would keep pace with wage rises. It comes amid warnings that the triple lock system is unsustainable in its current form.\n\nIn terms of a Green Party government's stewardship of the economy, its manifesto says it's \"prepared to borrow to invest. It suggests the other major parties are \"trapped in a self-imposed fiscal straitjacket\" and following a \"false economy\".\n\nJustifying its stance, the party says investing in the climate now would \"save vast costs in the future\". It adds that raising public services to a \"decent\" standard and ensuring infrastructure is \"fit-for-purpose\" are crucial for the future.\n\nBut, it also acknowledges that public spending can only go so far before it triggers \"dangerous levels of inflation\". So, the party's \"overriding fiscal rule\" would be to ensure inflation is not unduly increased by a Green government's expenditure.\n\nSince the Lib Dems made several ambitious green housing pledges in their manifesto, all eyes have been on what the Green Party would announce. Its manifesto goes slightly further.\n\nThe party wants all new homes to meet Passivhaus - or equivalent - standards, and says new builds must come with solar panels and heat pumps \"where appropriate\". Existing properties will go through a 10-year \"street-by-street\" retrofitting programme to boost insulation to EPC B standard or above (at a cost of \u00a329bn). They will also be able to benefit from incentives to install low-carbon heating systems (costing \u00a39bn). The party says it will create 150,000 new social homes a year through new construction and refurbishing older housing stock, whilst ending 'Right to Buy' so that social housing is kept \"in perpetuity\" for local communities.\n\nFor renters, the Green Party plans to allow local authorities to be able to control rents if they become unaffordable for local people. It wants to ban no-fault evictions, and to give tenants the right to demand energy efficiency improvements. These improvements would be funded by taxpayer loans, which would be repaid by landlords. The party has also committed to creating \"private residential tenancy boards\" that would provide an \"informal, cheap and speedy\" forum to resolve disputes between tenants and landlords.\n\nIn terms of property taxes, the party aspires to move away from \"regressive\" council tax and towards a Land Value Tax, which would ensure that owners of the \"most valuable and largest land holdings\" would pay the most. It would progress towards this goal by first re-evaluating council tax bands and then undertaking a survey of all landholdings.\n\nThe Green Party says it wants to make the financial services sector \"a force for good\" by directing finance towards progressive businesses. It says all UK banking licence holders will have to present an investment strategy that will set out a clear route to divestment from fossil fuel-related assets by 2030. Non-bank financial institutions, such as pension funds, will also have to remove fossil fuel assets from their portfolios by the end of the decade.\n\nIt also wants to alter the Bank of England's mandate so that its efforts are centred on funding a transition towards a sustainable economy and mapping out a greener banking system - alongside its existing responsibility to keep inflation in check.\n\nIndividual investors would also be encouraged to move away from fossil fuel equities under a Green government. The Financial Conduct Authority (FCA) would be tasked with developing goals to eliminate all fossil fuel-related stocks from the UK stock market. It would also bar any new fossil fuel listings or share allocations.\n\nElsewhere, the party says it would set up regional mutual banks in a bid to \"drive investment in decarbonisation and local economic sustainability\". Communities would also be given ownership of \"local sustainable energy infrastructure\", like wind farms.\n\nAs you'd imagine from the Green manifesto, there are a lot of commitments to move the UK towards a \"zero-carbon society\". For example, the party wants wind to provide around 70% of the nation's energy by 2030.\n\nThe party also wants to bring nuclear power to an end and invest in the country's energy storage capacity and more efficient electricity distribution. Its community ownership model (see above) would lower energy bills, it claims, by allowing local areas to sell excess energy.\n\nHowever, its energy bill cutting measures are almost-entirely focused Are you really being honest that you can raise all of this?\"\n\nDenyer answered: \"Our manifesto is fully costed; I think the Green Party was more ready for this election than many others.\n\n\"We do have ambitious spending commitments and the party is the only one being honest about what is needed to change things for this county.\"\n\nFollowing the Prime Minister's revelation that he went without Sky TV to fund his private education, one person asked: \"Rishi Sunak said that he had to go without Sky TV when he was growing up, what did you have to go without?\"\n\nDenyer replied: \"I also went without Sky TV, my parents had second-hand cars. I'm not trying to elicit any tears here as I had a fairly comfortable childhood.\"\n\nRamsay said: \"I had a similar upbringing,\" before jokingly adding, \"I went without a pet for most of my childhood.\"\n\nAfter the conference, The Argus caught up with Brighton Pavilion candidate Sian Berry, who hopes to take over from Caroline Lucas as Green MP.\n\nSian Berry, left, hopes to take over from former MP Caroline Lucas, right, as Green MP for Brighton Pavilion (Image: Lucy Hunt / The Argus)\n\nSian told us about the key issues Brighton voters have raised on the doorsteps, such as the NHS, renting conditions and sewage.\n\nShe said: \"We hear about the NHS a lot and the difficulty they have accessing it.\n\n\"They do not want the values and reputation of the NHS to be undermined.\n\n\"We are passionate about maintaining the NHS as a public service.\"\n\nShe also spoke highly of former Brighton Pavilion MP, Caroline Lucas.\n\nShe said: \"She has been this very strong, independent voice for the city, and an incredible advocate in Pavilion.\n\n\"She has been a great constituency MP.\"\n\nThe Argus also spoke to Adrian Ramsay about the Greens' prospects in Brighton and Hove.\n\nGreen Party co-leaders Carla Denyer and Adrian Ramsay (Image: Lucy Hunt / The Argus)\n\nHe said: \"The Green Party vote in Brighton has increased at each election.\n\n\"Caroline Lucas has shown that she is a really strong constituency MP, representing residents as well as shifting the dial of the debate in Parliament to press for action.\n\n\"Sian will be an equally strong Green MP. {"uri": "8174359046", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-12", "time": "13:39:53", "dateTime": "2024-06-12T13:39:53Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-12T13:38:56Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.5607843399047852, "url": "https://www.belfasttelegraph.co.uk/news/uk/the-green-partys-general-election-manifesto-at-a-glance/a1039123413.html", "title": "The Green Party's General Election manifesto at a glance", "body": "Here are some of the headline policy proposals from the Greens.\n\n- The economy\n\nThe Green Party has pledged to raise taxes for the wealthiest in society and mend \"broken Britain\" in its election manifesto.\n\nA tax on multimillionaires and billionaires will be used to raise additional revenue of between \u00a330-70 billion to help fund improvements to health, housing, transport and the green economy, the party said.\n\nIn its manifesto, the Greens also said \"privatisation has failed\" and promised to bring water companies, railways and the five big retail energy companies into state ownership.\n\nOther measures include a pledge to invest \u00a340 billion a year to shift to a green economy, a \u00a312.4 billion investment pledge in green skills and training, and a carbon tax to drive down emissions - all during the next parliament.\n\n- Energy\n\nThe Green Party said it will \"accelerate clean energy investment and delivery\" by cancelling recent oil and gas licences such as those for the Rosebank oil and gas field, removing all oil and gas subsidies and stopping all new fossil fuel extraction projects in the UK.\n\nThe party also said it wants to see \"the phase-out of all nuclear energy\" and will push for wind to provide 70% of UK energy by 2030.\n\n- Nature\n\nOn nature, the party has outlined extensive plans to protect and restore the UK's environment by \"giving rights to nature itself\".\n\nThe party will work to end the sewage scandal, extend people's access to nature with a new English Right to Roam Act, and end the emergency authorisation of bee-killing pesticides.\n\nOn protecting animals and habitats, elected MPs will call for an end to all blood sports, badger culling, factory farming, routine use of antibiotics in farm animals and close confinement in cages.\n\n- Farming\n\nFor more on agriculture, the Greens have pledged to \"work with farmers and other stakeholders to transform our food and farming system\".\n\nTheir manifesto includes measures such as almost tripling financial support for farmers to transition to nature-friendly farming, improving biodiversity and soil health, and linking farm payments to reduced use of pesticides.\n\n- Housing\n\nThe party has vowed to provide \"fairer, greener homes for all\", signalling that it wants to make the UK's housing crisis one of its key priorities for the next Parliament.\n\nUnder their plans, housebuilders would have to include solar panels and heat pumps on all new homes where appropriate and \u00a333 billion would be invested in insulating homes and other buildings over the next five years.\n\nThe party also promised to provide 150,000 new social homes every year and a \"fair deal for renters\" by giving powers to local authorities to introduce rent control measures.\n\n- Health and social care\n\nOn health, the Greens said they want to ensure a \"fully public, properly funded health and social care system\", promising to spend \u00a350 billion per year by 2030.\n\nThe manifesto said elected MPs will push for a year-on-year reduction in waiting lists, guaranteed access to an NHS dentist, guaranteed rapid access to a GP, and an immediate boost to the pay of NHS staff.\n\nOther areas the party will focus on include increasing funding for mental health care and support a change in the law to legalise assisted dying for those with terminal diseases.\n\nOn social care, the party has pledged to introduce free personal care, increase carer pay rates and introduce a career structure for the sector.\n\n- Workers' rights\n\nIn the manifesto, the party said it plans to \"defend and extend workers' rights\" as it outlined measures such as repealing \"anti-union\" legislation and introducing a maximum 10:1 pay ratio.\n\nThe party will also push to increase the minimum wage to \u00a315 an hour no matter the age - up from its current rate of \u00a311.44 an hour for those aged 21 or over - and will move to introduce a four-day working week.\n\n- Welfare\n\nIn the manifesto's section on social support, the party pledges to \"tackle the cost-of-living crisis for the poorest in our society\".\n\nMeasures include increasing Universal Credit and legacy benefits by \u00a340 a week, abolishing the two-child benefit cap, introducing a universal basic income and giving disability benefits an immediate 5% uplift.\n\n- Transport\n\nThe Greens' plans to cut carbon emissions include championing \"better, cheaper public transport\" by increasing annual public subsidies for rail and bus travel to \u00a310bn and investing an additional \u00a319 billion to improve public transport by the end of the next Parliament.\n\nOther measures include a focus on active travel, with the party pledging to invest \u00a32.5bn a year in new cycleways and footpaths, and reducing traffic in residential areas to boost community use.\n\nOn aviation, elected MPs will push for a frequent-flyer levy, a ban on domestic flights for journeys that would take less than three hours by train and a halt to the expansion of new airport capacity.\n\n- Education\n\nThe Greens said the current education system \"operates like a production line rather than valuing their individual qualities\".\n\nThe manifesto said elected MPs will champion an increase in school funding of \u00a38 billion, ending tuition fees for higher education students, stopping high-stakes testing at schools and abolishing Ofsted.\n\n- Arts and culture\n\nGreen MPs will push for a \u00a35 billion investment to support community sports, arts and culture, the manifesto said.\n\nThe party also backs efforts to keep local sports facilities, museums, theatres, libraries and art galleries open and thriving as well as ending VAT on cultural activities.\n\n- Media reform\n\nThe manifesto said the current media landscape is \"skewed by the dominance of billionaire and big-tech ownership\" with the aim of maximising profits.\n\nThe party said it will push for a change in the law so that no single individual or company can own more than 20% of any media market and introduce all reforms proposed in the second part of the 2012 Leveson Report.\n\n- Foreign policy\n\nThe Green Party said it will seek to rejoin the European Union \"as soon as the political conditions are right\" as it unveiled its foreign policy objectives.\n\nIn the manifesto, the party said UK foreign policy \"should be based on shared commitments to democracy, peace, global solidarity and the protection of human rights\" as it pledged continued support for Ukraine against the Russian invasion.\n\nOn Israel and Palestine, the party said it will push for an immediate bilateral ceasefire as well as a \"durable political solution\".\n\nOn nuclear weapons, the party said it would see the UK begin the process of dismantling its nuclear weapons, cancel the Trident programme and remove all foreign nuclear weapons from UK soil.\n\nAnd on supporting Global South countries to tackle the climate crisis, the Greens back an increase to international aid to 1% of gross national income (GNI) and an increase to climate finance to 1.5% by 2033.\n\n- Rights and freedoms\n\nThe manifesto said the party will \"defend the Human Rights Act, the UK's membership of the European Convention on Human Rights and continued direct access to convention rights in the domestic courts\".\n\nOn political reform, the party will replace the first past the post system for parliamentary elections with a fair and proportional voting system, replace the House of Lords with an elected second chamber and provide votes for 16-year-olds.\n\nThe party said it will also campaign to end violence against women and girls, scrap legislation that erodes the right to protest and free expression, and campaign for the right of self-identification for trans and non-binary people.\n\nOther headline pledges including working to renew England's crumbling court system with a \u00a32.5bn investment and giving Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland the choice to make their \"own decisions about their relationship with the rest of the United Kingdom\".\n\n- Immigration\n\nIn its manifesto, the Green Party said it welcomes \"the contributions that migrants and refugees make to British society\" and would support safe routes to sanctuary for those fleeing persecution.\n\nThe party also said it will replace a \"dysfunctional Home Office\" with a new Department of Migration, end immigration detention, and allow asylum seekers to work {"uri": "8174351208", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-12", "time": "13:35:14", "dateTime": "2024-06-12T13:35:14Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-12T13:34:06Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.5568627715110779, "url": "https://jerseyeveningpost.com/morenews/uknews/2024/06/12/the-green-partys-general-election-manifesto-at-a-glance/", "title": "The Green Party's General Election manifesto at a glance", "body": "The Green Party have launched their General Election manifesto, with promises to stop \"all new fossil fuel projects\", overhaul the tax system and give \"rights to nature itself\".\n\nHere are some of the headline policy proposals from the Greens.\n\n- The economy\n\nThe Green Party has pledged to raise taxes for the wealthiest in society and mend \"broken Britain\" in its election manifesto.\n\nA tax on multimillionaires and billionaires will be used to raise additional revenue of between \u00a330-70 billion to help fund improvements to health, housing, transport and the green economy, the party said.\n\nIn its manifesto, the Greens also said \"privatisation has failed\" and promised to bring water companies, railways and the five big retail energy companies into state ownership.\n\nOther measures include a pledge to invest \u00a340 billion a year to shift to a green economy, a \u00a312.4 billion investment pledge in green skills and training, and a carbon tax to drive down emissions - all during the next parliament.\n\nThe Green Party said it will \"accelerate clean energy investment and delivery\" by cancelling recent oil and gas licences such as those for the Rosebank oil and gas field, removing all oil and gas subsidies and stopping all new fossil fuel extraction projects in the UK.\n\nThe party also said it wants to see \"the phase-out of all nuclear energy\" and will push for wind to provide 70% of UK energy by 2030.\n\n- Nature\n\nOn nature, the party has outlined extensive plans to protect and restore the UK's environment by \"giving rights to nature itself\".\n\nThe party will work to end the sewage scandal, extend people's access to nature with a new English Right to Roam Act, and end the emergency authorisation of bee-killing pesticides.\n\nOn protecting animals and habitats, elected MPs will call for an end to all blood sports, badger culling, factory farming, routine use of antibiotics in farm animals and close confinement in cages.\n\nFor more on agriculture, the Greens have pledged to \"work with farmers and other stakeholders to transform our food and farming system\".\n\nTheir manifesto includes measures such as almost tripling financial support for farmers to transition to nature-friendly farming, improving biodiversity and soil health, and linking farm payments to reduced use of pesticides.\n\n- Housing\n\nThe party has vowed to provide \"fairer, greener homes for all\", signalling that it wants to make the UK's housing crisis one of its key priorities for the next Parliament.\n\nUnder their plans, housebuilders would have to include solar panels and heat pumps on all new homes where appropriate and \u00a333 billion would be invested in insulating homes and other buildings over the next five years.\n\nThe party also promised to provide 150,000 new social homes every year and a \"fair deal for renters\" by giving powers to local authorities to introduce rent control measures.\n\n- Health and social care\n\nOn health, the Greens said they want to ensure a \"fully public, properly funded health and social care system\", promising to spend \u00a350 billion per year by 2030.\n\nThe manifesto said elected MPs will push for a year-on-year reduction in waiting lists, guaranteed access to an NHS dentist, guaranteed rapid access to a GP, and an immediate boost to the pay of NHS staff.\n\nOn social care, the party has pledged to introduce free personal care, increase carer pay rates and introduce a career structure for the sector.\n\n- Workers' rights\n\nIn the manifesto, the party said it plans to \"defend and extend workers' rights\" as it outlined measures such as repealing \"anti-union\" legislation and introducing a maximum 10:1 pay ratio.\n\nThe party will also push to increase the minimum wage to \u00a315 an hour no matter the age - up from its current rate of \u00a311.44 an hour for those aged 21 or over - and will move to introduce a four-day working week.\n\n- Welfare\n\nIn the manifesto's section on social support, the party pledges to \"tackle the cost-of-living crisis for the poorest in our society\".\n\nMeasures include increasing Universal Credit and legacy benefits by \u00a340 a week, abolishing the two-child benefit cap, introducing a universal basic income and giving disability benefits an immediate 5% uplift.\n\n- Transport\n\nThe Greens' plans to cut carbon emissions include championing \"better, cheaper public transport\" by increasing annual public subsidies for rail and bus travel to \u00a310bn and investing an additional \u00a319 billion to improve public transport by the end of the next Parliament.\n\nOther measures include a focus on active travel, with the party pledging to invest \u00a32.5bn a year in new cycleways and footpaths, and reducing traffic in residential areas to boost community use.\n\nOn aviation, elected MPs will push for a frequent-flyer levy, a ban on domestic flights for journeys that would take less than three hours by train and a halt to the expansion of new airport capacity.\n\nThe Greens said the current education system \"operates like a production line rather than valuing their individual qualities\".\n\nThe manifesto said elected MPs will champion an increase in school funding of \u00a38 billion, ending tuition fees for higher education students, stopping high-stakes testing at schools and abolishing Ofsted.\n\n- Arts and culture\n\nGreen MPs will push for a \u00a35 billion investment to support community sports, arts and culture, the manifesto said.\n\nThe party also backs efforts to keep local sports facilities, museums, theatres, libraries and art galleries open and thriving as well as ending VAT on cultural activities.\n\n- Media reform\n\nThe manifesto said the current media landscape is \"skewed by the dominance of billionaire and big-tech ownership\" with the aim of maximising profits.\n\nThe party said it will push for a change in the law so that no single individual or company can own more than 20% of any media market and introduce all reforms proposed in the second part of the 2012 Leveson Report.\n\n- Foreign policy\n\nThe Green Party said it will seek to rejoin the European Union \"as soon as the political conditions are right\" as it unveiled its foreign policy objectives.\n\nIn the manifesto, the party said UK foreign policy \"should be based on shared commitments to democracy, peace, global solidarity and the protection of human rights\" as it pledged continued support for Ukraine against the Russian invasion.\n\nOn Israel and Palestine, the party said it will push for an immediate bilateral ceasefire as well as a \"durable political solution\".\n\nOn nuclear weapons, the party said it would see the UK begin the process of dismantling its nuclear weapons, cancel the Trident programme and remove all foreign nuclear weapons from UK soil.\n\nAnd on supporting Global South countries to tackle the climate crisis, the Greens back an increase to international aid to 1% of gross national income (GNI) and an increase to climate finance to 1.5% by 2033.\n\n- Rights and freedoms\n\nThe manifesto said the party will \"defend the Human Rights Act, the UK's membership of the European Convention on Human Rights and continued direct access to convention rights in the domestic courts\".\n\nOn political reform, the party will replace the first past the post system for parliamentary elections with a fair and proportional voting system, replace the House of Lords with an elected second chamber and provide votes for 16-year-olds.\n\nThe party said it will also campaign to end violence against women and girls, scrap legislation that erodes the right to protest and free expression, and campaign for the right of self-identification for trans and non-binary people.\n\nOther headline pledges including working to renew England's crumbling court system with a \u00a32.5bn investment and giving Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland the choice to make their \"own decisions about their relationship with the rest of the United Kingdom\".\n\nThe party also said it will replace a \"dysfunctional Home Office\" with a new Department of Migration, end immigration detention, and allow asylum seekers to work while their Here are some of its main points.\n\nMore tax, more spend\n\nUnlike the Conservatives and Labour, the Greens are unapologetic in opting out of what they call a \"race to the bottom\" on tax. Taxes would go up under their policies - though mainly for wealthier people.\n\nTo finance a range of public policies including insulating buildings, higher benefits and more money to the NHS and public spending, the party would levy a wealth tax on people worth \u00a310m or more, increase national insurance, and put capital gains tax at the same rate as income tax.\n\nDo all the sums add up? At the launch event the party's co-leader Adrian Ramsay said the manifesto was fully costed. There is not, however, a page of costings in the document.\n\nBuild, build, build\n\nThe launch event was opened by the party's Brighton Pavilion candidate, Si\u00e2n Berry, who noted that she was and had always been a renter, \"at the mercy of a system that can turn on me at any moment\".\n\nThe Greens' answers include building more social rent homes, acquiring more from the private sector, insulating homes so they are warmer, rent controls, and letting councils requisition empty properties or those without proper insulation.\n\nThere is also a section in the manifesto about ensuring any new homes have access to sufficient local infrastructure and no overly adverse environmental impacts. This could be seen as a nimby's charter for a party that runs or co-runs a series of councils - something denied by Ramsay and his co-leader Carla Denyer.\n\nTackling the cost of living\n\nDenyer said the manifesto aimed to address \"an economy where most people are working harder and yet getting poorer\". As well as investment in areas such as skills training, policies include a \u00a315-an-hour minimum wage, improved employment rights and a four-day working week.\n\nOn social security, plans include a \u00a340 weekly upgrade to universal credit and parallel legacy benefits, abolishing the two-child benefit cap and the bedroom tax, and in the longer term, introducing a universal basic income.\n\nA green society\n\nThe party has long moved on from almost entirely campaigning on nature and the environment, but this remains a priority.\n\nPolicies in the manifesto include phasing out fossil fuels and nuclear energy, and aiming for wind to provide 70% of the UK's electricity by 2030. Farmers would be heavily incentivised to promote biodiversity and use fewer pesticides.\n\nOn transport, there is little of the \"plan for drivers\", with instead mass spending on public transport and a promised \u00a32.5bn a year for building walking and cycling infrastructure.\n\nWe're sober and sensible, really\n\nWhile the Greens' policies are certainly more radical than those of the bigger parties, the manifesto launch was notably short on controversy or contentious ideas, as exemplified by the sensible figures of Denyer and Ramsay.\n\nA question about abandoning nuclear weapons was answered less in terms of a moral choice than one of cost and practicality; asked if the Greens believed jailed climate protesters should be freed, Denyer said it was not up to politicians to get Green Party manifesto for 'grasping reality' of climate crisis", "body": "Green groups praised the party's stance on taxing the rich but the Institute for Fiscal Studies said its plans will come with a 'real economic cost'.\n\nThe Green Party has \"grasped the reality\" of the climate crisis, environmental groups have said as many welcomed its new manifesto.\n\nThe party pledged to stop all new fossil fuel projects, tax the wealthy and \"mend broken Britain\" as it launched its manifesto in Hove, East Sussex, on Wednesday.\n\nAccording to the document, elected Green MPs will push for measures that restore the UK's depleted nature, increase the energy efficiency of homes and spend \u00a350 billion per year on health and social care by 2030.\n\nEnvironmental groups praised the party for its \"honesty\" around the scale of changes needed to tackle the climate crisis as well as its proposals to tax the rich.\n\nAs the country prepares to go to the polls on July 4, many believe climate and nature have been sidelined during the campaign, with the economy, national security, immigration and the NHS dominating debates.\n\nMike Childs, head of policy at Friends of the Earth, said: \"The Green Party is so far the only political party that has grasped the reality that unless we properly invest in reducing carbon emissions from our homes, industries, farming and transport, it will be impossible to avoid the huge financial and human costs of the climate crisis in the future.\n\n\"By taxing the wealthiest in society and introducing carbon taxes on polluting companies, the Greens aim to benefit the poorest in society through fixing the UK's damp, heat-leaking homes, improving our public transport system and enabling farmers to earn a decent living from green farming, as well as properly funding essential services such as the NHS.\n\n\"Honesty is essential to regaining trust in politics, so hats off to the Green Party for having the honesty to say that we can't fix our broken energy system, ailing public services and protect our planet without spending much more money.\"\n\nDr Doug Parr, Greenpeace UK policy director, said the manifesto \"clearly recognises\" that there is no long-term prosperity or security for anyone without tackling the climate and nature crisis.\n\n\"Both the Green Party and the Lib Dems have now drawn a clear line in the sand between them and the Labour Party on the common sense notion that the very richest people should pay more to fix our crumbling country,\" he said.\n\n\"Not only do the super-rich have the broadest shoulders, but they are also responsible for the most climate damage. It's only right that they pay more towards upgrading our homes, infrastructure and public services.\"\n\nThe Institute for Fiscal Studies (IFS) warned the party's manifesto proposals will come with a \"real economic cost\" and increase disincentives to work.\n\nIts deputy director, Helen Miller, described some plans - such as closing loopholes within inheritance tax - as \"sensible\" but added: \"While a recurrent wealth tax could raise revenue, it would be tough to implement\".\n\n\"It is clear where the Green Party's ambitions lie - a much bigger role for the state, better-funded public services and, of course, a swifter transition to net zero,\" she said.\n\n\"It is unlikely that the specific tax-raising measures they propose to help achieve all this would raise the sorts of sums they claim - and certainly not without real economic cost.\"\n\nElsewhere, Max Wakefield, co-director of climate charity Possible, joined the praise for the Green Party, saying: \"It's great to see a manifesto which acknowledges that we need to be going further and faster on climate, containing a huge raft of policies designed to cut the cost of living while cutting carbon.\n\n\"What this manifesto really gets right is recognising that climate cuts across all areas of our society and our economy, and you can see this embedded in policy from housing, to the economy, to transport, rather than boxed off in its own chapter with vague targets and ambitions.\"\n\nLouise Hutchins, head of policy at the UK Green Building Council, said: \"So far, this election hasn't been a 'climate election' despite the dire warnings from scientists.\n\n\"This manifesto is a useful contribution to driving the issue up the agenda given the next government will be the last capable of bringing in It said it would raise up to \u00a380bn from a carbon tax to be set initially at \u00a3120 per tonne of carbon emitted, while adding that it was \"prepared to borrow to invest\" further.\n\n\"Our manifesto is based on investing to mend broken Britain and offer real hope and real change,\" Carla Denyer, co-leader of the party, said at the party's manifesto launch in Brighton on Wednesday.\n\n\"At the heart of this would be a tax on the very richest, the top 1 per cent of people, requiring them to pay a bit more into the pot,\" she said. \"From the Tories and Labour, we've been hearing a race to the bottom on tax.\"\n\nThe party has sought to expand beyond its core policy focus of the environment and climate to become a foil to Labour, stealing away leftwing voters who feel uninspired or unsatisfied with Sir Keir Starmer's position on issues ranging from the conflict in Gaza to taxation and public investment.\n\nThe Labour party ruled out imposing a dedicated wealth tax last year and has also said it has no plans to bring capital gains tax in line with income tax rates. {"uri": "8174351181", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-12", "time": "13:34:55", "dateTime": "2024-06-12T13:34:55Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-12T13:34:05Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.529411792755127, "url": "https://www.expressandstar.com/news/uk-news/2024/06/12/the-green-partys-general-election-manifesto-at-a-glance/", "title": "The Green Party's General Election manifesto at a glance", "body": "Here are some of the headline policy proposals from the Greens.\n\nThe Green Party have launched their General Election manifesto, with promises to stop \"all new fossil fuel projects\", overhaul the tax system and give \"rights to nature itself\".\n\nHere are some of the headline policy proposals from the Greens.\n\n- The economy\n\nThe Green Party has pledged to raise taxes for the wealthiest in society and mend \"broken Britain\" in its election manifesto.\n\nA tax on multimillionaires and billionaires will be used to raise additional revenue of between \u00a330-70 billion to help fund improvements to health, housing, transport and the green economy, the party said.\n\nIn its manifesto, the Greens also said \"privatisation has failed\" and promised to bring water companies, railways and the five big retail energy companies into state ownership.\n\nOther measures include a pledge to invest \u00a340 billion a year to shift to a green economy, a \u00a312.4 billion investment pledge in green skills and training, and a carbon tax to drive down emissions - all during the next parliament.\n\n- Energy\n\nThe Green Party said it will \"accelerate clean energy investment and delivery\" by cancelling recent oil and gas licences such as those for the Rosebank oil and gas field, removing all oil and gas subsidies and stopping all new fossil fuel extraction projects in the UK.\n\nThe party also said it wants to see \"the phase-out of all nuclear energy\" and will push for wind to provide 70% of UK energy by 2030.\n\n- Nature\n\nOn nature, the party has outlined extensive plans to protect and restore the UK's environment by \"giving rights to nature itself\".\n\nThe party will work to end the sewage scandal, extend people's access to nature with a new English Right to Roam Act, and end the emergency authorisation of bee-killing pesticides.\n\nOn protecting animals and habitats, elected MPs will call for an end to all blood sports, badger culling, factory farming, routine use of antibiotics in farm animals and close confinement in cages.\n\n- Farming\n\nFor more on agriculture, the Greens have pledged to \"work with farmers and other stakeholders to transform our food and farming system\".\n\nTheir manifesto includes measures such as almost tripling financial support for farmers to transition to nature-friendly farming, improving biodiversity and soil health, and linking farm payments to reduced use of pesticides.\n\n- Housing\n\nThe party has vowed to provide \"fairer, greener homes for all\", signalling that it wants to make the UK's housing crisis one of its key priorities for the next Parliament.\n\nUnder their plans, housebuilders would have to include solar panels and heat pumps on all new homes where appropriate and \u00a333 billion would be invested in insulating homes and other buildings over the next five years.\n\nThe party also promised to provide 150,000 new social homes every year and a \"fair deal for renters\" by giving powers to local authorities to introduce rent control measures.\n\n- Health and social care\n\nOn health, the Greens said they want to ensure a \"fully public, properly funded health and social care system\", promising to spend \u00a350 billion per year by 2030.\n\nThe manifesto said elected MPs will push for a year-on-year reduction in waiting lists, guaranteed access to an NHS dentist, guaranteed rapid access to a GP, and an immediate boost to the pay of NHS staff.\n\nOther areas the party will focus on include increasing funding for mental health care and support a change in the law to legalise assisted dying for those with terminal diseases.\n\nOn social care, the party has pledged to introduce free personal care, increase carer pay rates and introduce a career structure for the sector.\n\n- Workers' rights\n\nIn the manifesto, the party said it plans to \"defend and extend workers' rights\" as it outlined measures such as repealing \"anti-union\" legislation and introducing a maximum 10:1 pay ratio.\n\nThe party will also push to increase the minimum wage to \u00a315 an hour no matter the age - up from its current rate of \u00a311.44 an hour for those aged 21 or over - and will move to introduce a four-day working week.\n\n- Welfare\n\nIn the manifesto's section on social support, the party pledges to \"tackle the cost-of-living crisis for the poorest in our society\".\n\nMeasures include increasing Universal Credit and legacy benefits by \u00a340 a week, abolishing the two-child benefit cap, introducing a universal basic income and giving disability benefits an immediate 5% uplift.\n\n- Transport\n\nThe Greens' plans to cut carbon emissions include championing \"better, cheaper public transport\" by increasing annual public subsidies for rail and bus travel to \u00a310bn and investing an additional \u00a319 billion to improve public transport by the end of the next Parliament.\n\nOther measures include a focus on active travel, with the party pledging to invest \u00a32.5bn a year in new cycleways and footpaths, and reducing traffic in residential areas to boost community use.\n\nOn aviation, elected MPs will push for a frequent-flyer levy, a ban on domestic flights for journeys that would take less than three hours by train and a halt to the expansion of new airport capacity.\n\n- Education\n\nThe Greens said the current education system \"operates like a production line rather than valuing their individual qualities\".\n\nThe manifesto said elected MPs will champion an increase in school funding of \u00a38 billion, ending tuition fees for higher education students, stopping high-stakes testing at schools and abolishing Ofsted.\n\n- Arts and culture\n\nGreen MPs will push for a \u00a35 billion investment to support community sports, arts and culture, the manifesto said.\n\nThe party also backs efforts to keep local sports facilities, museums, theatres, libraries and art galleries open and thriving as well as ending VAT on cultural activities.\n\n- Media reform\n\nThe manifesto said the current media landscape is \"skewed by the dominance of billionaire and big-tech ownership\" with the aim of maximising profits.\n\nThe party said it will push for a change in the law so that no single individual or company can own more than 20% of any media market and introduce all reforms proposed in the second part of the 2012 Leveson Report.\n\n- Foreign policy\n\nThe Green Party said it will seek to rejoin the European Union \"as soon as the political conditions are right\" as it unveiled its foreign policy objectives.\n\nIn the manifesto, the party said UK foreign policy \"should be based on shared commitments to democracy, peace, global solidarity and the protection of human rights\" as it pledged continued support for Ukraine against the Russian invasion.\n\nOn Israel and Palestine, the party said it will push for an immediate bilateral ceasefire as well as a \"durable political solution\".\n\nOn nuclear weapons, the party said it would see the UK begin the process of dismantling its nuclear weapons, cancel the Trident programme and remove all foreign nuclear weapons from UK soil.\n\nAnd on supporting Global South countries to tackle the climate crisis, the Greens back an increase to international aid to 1% of gross national income (GNI) and an increase to climate finance to 1.5% by 2033.\n\n- Rights and freedoms\n\nThe manifesto said the party will \"defend the Human Rights Act, the UK's membership of the European Convention on Human Rights and continued direct access to convention rights in the domestic courts\".\n\nOn political reform, the party will replace the first past the post system for parliamentary elections with a fair and proportional voting system, replace the House of Lords with an elected second chamber and provide votes for 16-year-olds.\n\nThe party said it will also campaign to end violence against women and girls, scrap legislation that erodes the right to protest and free expression, and campaign for the right of self-identification for trans and non-binary people.\n\nOther headline pledges including working to renew England's crumbling court system with a \u00a32.5bn investment and giving Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland the choice to make their \"own decisions about their relationship with the rest of the United Kingdom\".\n\n- Immigration\n\nIn its manifesto, the Green Party said it welcomes \"the contributions that migrants and refugees make to British society\" and would support safe routes to sanctuary for those fleeing persecution.\n\nThe party also said it will replace a \"dysfunctional Home Office\" with a new Department of Migration, end immigration detention, and allow asylum seekers to work "sim": 0.5215686559677124, "url": "https://www.businessgreen.com/news/4321719/green-party-manifesto-unveils-vision-reaching-net-zero-soon", "title": "Green Party Manifesto unveils vision for reaching net zero 'as soon as possible'", "body": "Greens' Manifesto sets out plans for major overhaul of tax system to raise revenues for public services, renewable power, and home insulation drive\n\nThe Green Party has promised to push the next government to accelerate the transition to net zero and reach decarbonisation goals at least a decade earlier than the current 2050 target, as it unveiled \"an ambitious but realistic plan for the future\" in its 2024 General Election manifesto.\n\nLaunching the manifesto in Brighton and Hove earlier today, the Greens unveiled a major reform package for the UK economy, backed by ambitious targets for clean energy, proposals for a new wealth tax, and a pledge to bring the UK's water industry, railways, and energy retail companies into public ownership.\n\nEye-catching pledges in the manifesto include a promise to push for the UK to rejoin the EU \"as soon as the political conditions are right\", legislate to ensure no single company or individual can own more than 20 per cent of the media market, and introduce a four-day working week.\n\nThe radical proposals come in addition to a plan to replace the UK's 'first past the post' voting system with a proportional system for electing MPs, introduce a fully elected House of Lords, and allow anyone over the age of 16 to vote.\n\nThe document also includes a raft of measures to boost Britain's green economy and enhance the natural world, through efforts such as ramping up renewable energy capacity, delivering a major national home insulation drive, and hiking taxes on carbon emissions.\n\nUnveiling the manifesto at Sussex Country Cricket Club's ground in Hove this morning, Green Party co-leader Carla Denyer said the plans were focused on \"mending broken Britain and offering real hope\" to the public.\n\n\"We plan to transform our economy for good,\" she said. \"To embrace a fairer, greener economy.\n\n\"Now is the moment to be ambitious - not unrealistic - but ambitious. To be clear about the kind of country we want to live in. About how broken our public services are and the action that's needed to fix them.\"\n\nAt the heart of the manifesto is the Party's vision for a \"fairer, greener tax system\", which sets out a raft of reforms aimed at raising tens of billions of pounds more a year to spend on the NHS, improved public services, and a nationwide home insulation drive.\n\nThese include plans for a new carbon tax on all businesses, starting at \u00a3120 a tonne and rising to \u00a3500 a tonne over 10 years. The tax would initially align existing fossil fuel taxes such as fuel duty, the Climate Change Levy, and payments under the UK's Emissions Trading Scheme (ETS), and could raise up to \u00a380bn a year, according to the manifesto.\n\nThe Party said it would not impose any further increases in the main rates of corporation tax, but it would \"initially\" maintain the current windfall tax on the profits of energy and oil and gas firms, in addition to introducing a new levy on banks following significant interest rate rises in recent years. Such moves could raise another \u00a39bn, it claimed.\n\nIt also proposes a raft of personal tax reforms it claims could raise between \u00a350bn and \u00a370bn a year for the Treasury. These include a new one per cent wealth tax on all assets worth more than \u00a310m, increasing to two per cent on assets above \u00a31bn, in addition to increases in capital gains tax, and an uptick in the National Insurance rate to eight per cent for those earning more than \u00a350,270 a year.\n\nThe revenue gained from new taxes on polluters and wealthiest households combined with borrowing would enable a \"game-changing\" transformation of the UK's health, housing, transport, and green infrastructure, the Party said.\n\nHeadline spending plans in the manifesto include an extra \u00a350bn a year on health and social care by 2030, and \u00a320bn in capital investment to bring \"crumbling\" hospitals, primary care buildings and equipment up to scratch.\n\nCrucially, the manifesto outlines plans to invest \u00a340bn a year in support of the shift to a greener economy, with \u00a312.4bn set aside for green skills training and \u00a32bn to for councils to help local businesses decarbonise.\n\nIt also sets out plans to spend \u00a329bn over the next five years to insulate homes nationwide up to a minimum Energy Performance Certificate (EPC) band B for energy efficiency, as well as provide \u00a34bn to insulate commercial and public buildings, and \u00a39bn to support the rollout of low carbon heating systems such as heat pumps.\n\nOn energy, the Greens are targeting 70 per cent of UK power supply from offshore and onshore wind by 2030, with goals to ramp up offshore wind capacity to 80GW, onshore wind to 53GW and solar to 100GW by as soon as 2035, all backed by further investment in energy storage capacity, according to the manifesto.\n\nOther clean energy policies include encouraging community ownership in local clean energy infrastructure such as wind farms backed by greater access to government funding.\n\nHowever, in stark contrast to Labour and the Conservatives, the Greens said they wanted to see a phase-out of nuclear energy in the UK, which they argue is unsafe, too slow to develop, and \"much more expensive than renewables\".\n\nAt the same time, the Party pledged to cancel all recent licenses for new oil and gas drilling in UK waters - including the controversial Rosebank project in the North Sea - while banning all new fossil fuel extraction projects and removing all subsidies for the oil and gas industry.\n\nThe manifesto also proposes a carbon tax on all fossil fuel imports and domestic extraction, based on greenhouse gas emissions produced when the fuel is burned, the manifesto states.\n\nProposed investments in transport include \u00a319bn for improving public transport and \u00a32.5bn a year for new cycleways and footpaths over the next five years, in addition to increasing public subsidies for rail and bus travel to \u00a310bn by the end of the next Parliament and offering free bus travel for under-18s.\n\nThe Party would also give local authorities more control over funding for improve bus services, back schemes to reduce traffic in residential areas, and adopt a target for half of all trips in England's towns and cities to be undertaken by foot or bike by 2030, according to the manifesto.\n\nThe Greens would seek to implement a frequent flyer levy on those traveling frequently by plane - a proposal explicitly rules out by the Conservatives in their manifesto yesterday - as well as introduce a ban on domestic flights for journeys that would take less than three hours by train and a moratorium on the expansion of new airport capacity across the country.\n\nNature pledges include introducing a new Rights of Nature Act, extending public rights to access green space with a new Right to Roam Act, and bringing in a new \"Clean Air (Human Rights) Act\". It also promises to ban bee-killing pesticides and deliver the UK's commitment to protect 30 per cent of land and seas by 2030.\n\nFinancial support for farmers to support nature-friendly agriculture would also be tripled, the Party said.\n\nElsewhere, the manifesto emphasises the Greens' commitment to the UK's international climate obligations, while promising to increase climate finance for the Global South to 1.5 per cent of Britain's gross national income by 2033 and provide further funding to the new international Loss and Damage Fund that is scheduled to become operational in the coming year.\n\nApeaking at the launch event this morning, Green Party co-leader Adrian Ramsay said there was a need to \"be bold and ambitious\" to invest in boosting Britain's economy and stressed that \"protecting our climate and nature lies at the heart of our policies\".\n\nThe Greens are fielding record numbers of candidates in next month's General Election, with hopes of potentially returning four or more MPs to Parliament, despite the Party's current sole MP Caroline Lucas stepping down.\n\n\"With more Green MPs in Parliament, we will stop Labour backtracking on any more of their promises,\" said Ramsay. \"We will push them to be braver, to be more ambitious, and to actually do what's necessary to fix our broken country and get us back on track.\"\n\nYou can now sign up to launch in East Sussex, co-leader Carla Denyer said their suggested \"overhaul\" of the tax system would see \"billions go into the country's collective pocket to pay for the future of our public with workers' income tax, and removing the upper earnings limit on national insurance.\n\nRead more:\n\nA guide to the Green Party\n\nFive questions leaders could face in tonight's Sky News event\n\nThe Greens believe this could raise up to \u00a370bn a year, with their plans to introduce a carbon tax adding another \u00a380bn to the government's coffers.\n\nBut unlike some of their they are committed to a \"fully public, properly funded health and social care system\" with an investment of \u00a350bn.\n\nBut more specifically, the party will push for a year-on-year reduction in People earning less than \u00a350,000 would not be affected,\" Ramsay said.\n\nThe tax overhaul would generate between \u00a350 billion and \u00a370 billion a year based on current prices, she estimated.\n\nThe Institute for Fiscal Studies said the Green Party's vision would \"see the size of government increase on an unprecedented scale\".\n\nTHE IFS He acknowledged the wealth tax would raise \"substantial sums\" but said borrowing would also have to increase.\n\n\"Even taking their figures at face value, overall borrowing would end up being around \u00a380 billion more per year and we could expect debt to increase over the next Parliament,\" they said .\n\nThe Green Party has admitted its policies are costly, but says it is \"worth it\" for better public services - starting with the NHS.\n\nThe party would invest \u00a350 billion in health and social care to \"defend and restore\" health services. The Conservatives, for comparison, have only committed to increasing spending in line with inflation.\n\nThe massive tax boost would enable \"a year-on-year reduction in waiting lists\" - currently at record levels - and guarantee access to an NHS dentist.\n\nThe system-wide salary increases include a 35% pay increase for junior doctors.\n\nThe party would also legislate for free personal care, modeled on that available in Scotland, and dedicate an extra \u00a33 billion to local authorities for children's social care.\n\nThe Greens have pledged to build 150,000 new genuinely affordable social homes each year and end the Right to Buy scheme.\n\nThe party would launch a massive \u00a348 billion renovation campaign, insulating homes and installing heat pumps. Denyer admitted the program was expensive, but said it would ultimately reduce energy bills.\n\n\"Warm, secure and affordable housing is something that millions of people in this country do not have,\" she added. \"[It is an] important, a basic element for a happy and successful life.\n\nOther plans include rent control and an end to no-fault evictions.\n\nThe Social Market Foundation welcomed this project as a step in the right direction.\n\n\"We welcome the Greens' plan for social housing which promises both more development of social housing and the maintenance of existing social housing stock by ending Help to Buy, as outlined in previous SMF research,\" said researcher Niamh O Regan.\n\n\"Their target to build 150,000 social homes would mean doubling current social housing construction - it's ambitious, but with social housing as the main objective of housing policy, it's possible. They do not, however, describe how this would be financed. past work of SMF suggested that fairness taxes could provide \u00a34 billion of funding each year for social housing.\"\n\nThe Greens would renationalise water and rail. It would also bring the big five retail energy companies into public ownership.\n\nLabor has pledged to renationalise the railways, clawing back private contracts as they expire. However, the party reversed its plan to nationalize water. Privatized water companies paid around \u00a3377 to shareholders for every hour polluted last year.\n\nThe Green Party would ban all new fossil fuel projects and cancel those recently licensed, such as Rosebank.\n\nFunding for renewable energy would come from a carbon tax, a levy on activities that lead to CO2 emissions. The party wants 70% of Britain's electricity to come from wind by 2030.\n\n\"A secure energy supply and action on the climate crisis go hand in hand,\" Ramsay said. They want to reach net zero \"years ahead\" of the current schedule.\n\nMike Childs, head of policy at Friends of the Earth, said urgency was needed.\n\n\"The Green Party is so far the only political party that has understood the reality that unless we invest properly in reducing carbon emissions from our homes, our industries, our agriculture and our transport, it will be impossible to avoid the enormous financial and human costs of the climate change crisis in the future,\" he said.\n\n\"Money spent on green growth allows us to permanently reduce energy bills, create long-term jobs in clean industries and stimulate the economy. \"\n\nBig Issue demands an end to poverty in this general election. So people who fly on holiday once a year would not be charged extra, because we're talking about frequent fliers, and actually just 15 per cent of the population take 70 per cent of the flights.\n\n\"So we're talking about business people or people who have wealth and fly frequently.\"\n\nHe said the Greens \"haven't set out a specific proposal for exactly how it would work\", but pointed to the NEF model as one example.\n\nHe added: \"The key here is the word frequent... the people who are flying all the time, not the ordinary family that's having one or two holidays a year\".\n\nMeanwhile, the Green Party said that it would invest heavily in public transport to make it easier and cheaper for people to go on holiday in Britain.\n\nTheir plans include bringing the railways back into public ownership, introducing free bus travel for under-18s and investing \u00a32.5 billion a year in new cycleways and footpaths.\n\nThe party would also ban all domestic flights where the journey would take less than three hours by train and halt the expansion of new airport capacity.\n\nAsked if he would ultimately like to see domestic flights scrapped completely, Mr Ramsay said: \"We've proposed our vision for the next five years, which is all you can do when there's a general election, to say what you think is achievable in the next parliament.\n\n\"But aviation has a high carbon impact, particularly from those who are frequent fliers, who are 15 per cent of people taking 70 per cent of the flights. {"uri": "8174570125", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-12", "time": "15:57:21", "dateTime": "2024-06-12T15:57:21Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-12T15:56:51Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.4823529422283173, "url": "https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/article/2024/jun/12/green-party-manifesto-labour-wealth-tax-public-services-keir-starmer", "title": "Who should hold the next prime minister to account? Our best hope lies with the Green party | George Monbiot", "body": "The party's manifesto, which pledges to use a wealth tax to revitalise our public services, shows it can push Labour to raise its ambitions\n\nAll governments betray the hopes of their supporters. But Labour is getting its betrayal in early. By ruling out a wealth tax and other measures that could fund our collapsing public services and our increasingly desperate care and welfare needs; by failing to denounce the unfolding genocide in Gaza; by remaining silent about the curtailment of our rights to protest; by breaking its promises on everything from a national care service to the abolition of the House of Lords and a right to roam, Keir Starmer's party appears to wear betrayal as a badge of honour. This country is desperate for change, but while Starmer mumbles the word in every sentence, he offers as little as he can get away with.\n\nWhy? Labour's anticipatory betrayal is motivated by anticipatory compliance. This means avoiding conflict with billionaire-owned media, the financial, property and fossil fuel sectors, by giving them what they want before they ask. You could call this approach \"political realism\". But the \"realistic\" result is a politics dominated by the sinister rich. Dysfunction and misrule are baked in.\n\nSo, whatever the size of Labour's likely majority, we will need countervailing forces in parliament. Though I have never been a member of any party, I believe our best hope lies with the Green party. It is the only one that has a chance of winning seats in this election that is not beholden to oligarchic power. Its new manifesto offers us everything a genuine Labour party, fit for the 21st century, should be promising.\n\nThe Greens propose immediately to bring the railways, water companies and the \"big five\" energy companies back into public ownership. They would guarantee access to an NHS dentist for everyone. They would greatly increase NHS funding, to reduce waiting times, build hospitals, improve primary care and restore pay levels for junior doctors and other staff. They would also greatly expand the provision of mental healthcare, and ensure that children and adolescents no longer need to wait years for an assessment. They'd ensure that all elderly people and people with disabilities have access to free personal care.\n\nThey want to address the shocking failures of the welfare state, increasing universal credit by \u00a340 a week, restoring the value of disability benefits, abolishing the iniquitous two-child benefit cap and scrapping the bedroom tax. They seek to end tuition fees and bring back grants for higher education, pour an extra \u00a38bn of investment into schools and \u00a35bn for special needs provision within mainstream education. They would increase public subsidies for rail and bus transport by \u00a310bn a year.\n\nThey also intend to repeal anti-union legislation and introduce a charter of workers' rights, to press for a maximum pay ratio within any organisation of 10:1, and to ensure all workers have full employment rights and protections. How did we end up with a situation in which none of this is Labour policy?\n\nThe Green party would also radically reform the housing sector, providing 150,000 new social homes a year, ensuring that social homes can no longer be sold off, and enabling local authorities to introduce rent controls. It would invest \u00a329bn over five years to insulate and improve housing, and give tenants the right to demand energy efficiency improvements from their landlords.\n\nThe Green manifesto proposes replacing the House of Lords with an elected chamber, introducing a fairer voting and party funding system, restoring the right to protest, ensuring no one can own more than 20% of the media and introducing a digital bill of rights to regulate social media providers.\n\nMost importantly, the Greens would confront the greatest predicament humankind has ever faced, by transforming our relationship with the natural world. This would include ending all new and recent oil and gas licences, introducing an escalating carbon tax and ensuring 70% of electricity comes from wind by 2030. Manufacturers of household white goods would have to offer 10-year warranties, there would be a comprehensive right to repair, and new green duties on all company directors. A right to roam would be accompanied by large-scale ecological protections, on land and at sea.\n\nAll this and more would be funded with a combination of a wealth tax, set at 1% a year for those with assets worth over \u00a310m, and 2% for those with assets above \u00a31bn; a reformed capital gains tax, bringing it into line with income tax levels; much fairer taxation of homes and property; and higher windfall taxes on oil and gas production, and on banks when excessive profits are made. It's everything the Labour party should be doing, in other words, but is too frightened to propose. No one expects the Greens to form the new government but we need them to hold Labour to account and press it to raise its level of ambition.\n\nThe Green party has a good chance of winning two seats in this election - Brighton Pavilion, where former party leader Caroline Lucas is standing down, and Bristol Central - and a smaller chance of winning a further two, Waveney Valley and North Herefordshire. If you live in one of these constituencies and wish to evict the Tories, this is the most effective way to use your vote. That's because, unlike most of us, you have a realistic chance of electing an MP who can speak for the people against plutocratic power.
